<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potassium-2.jpg"><img alt="Potassium-2.jpg" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="481" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Potassium-2.jpg/220px-Potassium-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Potassium-2.jpg/330px-Potassium-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Potassium-2.jpg/440px-Potassium-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div>Potassium pearls (in paraffin oil, ~5 mm each)</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff6666">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">pə-<span style="font-size:90%">TAS</span>-ee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
silvery gray</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r865336399">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<th style="text-align:right;"><sup>41</sup>K
</th>
<td style="text-align:right">6.730%

</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><td class="noprint" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff6666">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_potassium" title="Template:Infobox potassium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_potassium" title="Template talk:Infobox potassium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_potassium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Potassium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>K</b> (from <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">Neo-Latin</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/kalium#Latin" title="wikt:kalium">kalium</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 19. It was first isolated from <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>, the ashes of plants, from which its name derives. In the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, potassium is one of the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a>. All of the alkali metals have a single <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electron</a> in the outer electron shell, which is easily removed to create an ion with a positive charge – a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cation</a>, which combines with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anions</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>. Potassium in nature occurs only in ionic salts. Elemental potassium is a soft silvery-white alkali metal that <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidizes</a> rapidly in air and reacts vigorously with water, generating sufficient heat to ignite <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> emitted in the reaction, and burning with a lilac-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flame_color" title="Flame color">colored flame</a>.  It is found dissolved in sea water (which is 0.04% potassium by weight<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seawaterconcentration_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-seawaterconcentration-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>), and is part of many <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>.
</p><p>Potassium is chemically very similar to <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, the previous element in group 1 of the periodic table. They have a similar first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a>, which allows for each atom to give up its sole outer electron. That they are different elements that combine with the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anions</a> to make similar salts was suspected in 1702,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1702Suspect_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-1702Suspect-7">[7]</a></sup> and was proven in 1807 using <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>. Naturally occurring potassium is composed of three <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span></a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>. Traces of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> are found in all potassium, and it is the most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> in the human body.
</p><p>Potassium ions are vital for the functioning of all living cells. The transfer of potassium ions through nerve cell membranes is necessary for normal nerve transmission; potassium deficiency and excess can each result in numerous signs and symptoms, including an abnormal heart rhythm and various <a href="/wiki/Electrocardiography" title="Electrocardiography">electrocardiographic</a> abnormalities. Fresh fruits and vegetables are good dietary sources of potassium. The body responds to the influx of dietary potassium, which raises serum potassium levels, with a shift of potassium from outside to inside cells and an increase in potassium excretion by the kidneys.
</p><p>Most industrial applications of potassium exploit the high <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> in water of potassium compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Saltwater_soap" title="Saltwater soap">potassium</a> <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soaps</a>. Heavy crop production rapidly depletes the soil of potassium, and this can be remedied with agricultural fertilizers containing potassium, accounting for 95% of global potassium chemical production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g73_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-g73-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English name for the element <i>potassium</i> comes from the word "<a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> which refers to an early method of extracting various potassium salts: placing in a <i>pot</i> the <i>ash</i> of burnt wood or tree leaves, adding water, heating, and evaporating the solution. When <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> first isolated the pure element using <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> in 1807, he named it <i>potassium</i>, which he derived from the word potash.
</p><p>The symbol "K" stems from <i>kali</i>, itself from the root word <i><a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a></i>, which in turn comes from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">القَلْيَه</span>‎</i> <i>al-qalyah</i> "plant ashes."  In 1797, the German chemist  <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Klaproth</a> discovered "potash" in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Leucite" title="Leucite">leucite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepidolite" title="Lepidolite">lepidolite</a>, and realized that "potash" was not a product of plant growth but actually contained a new element, which he proposed to call <i>kali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1807, <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> produced the element via electrolysis: in 1809, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wilhelm_Gilbert" title="Ludwig Wilhelm Gilbert">Ludwig Wilhelm Gilbert</a> proposed the name <i>Kalium</i> for Davy's "potassium".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>  In 1814, the Swedish chemist  <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Berzelius</a> advocated the name <i>kalium</i> for potassium, with the chemical symbol "K".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The English and French speaking countries adopted Davy and Gay-Lussac/Thénard's name Potassium, while the Germanic countries adopted Gilbert/Klaproth's name Kalium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The "Gold Book" of the International Union of Physical and Applied Chemistry has designated the official chemical symbol  as <b>K</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:145px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flammenf%C3%A4rbungK.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="143" height="425" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Flammenf%C3%A4rbungK.png" width="143"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flammenf%C3%A4rbungK.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flame_test" title="Flame test">flame test</a> of potassium.</div></div></div>
<p>Potassium is the second least dense metal after <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>. It is a soft solid with a low <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, and can be easily cut with a knife. Freshly cut potassium is silvery in appearance, but it begins to tarnish toward gray immediately on exposure to air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g76_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-g76-15">[15]</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Flame_test" title="Flame test">flame test</a>, potassium and its compounds emit a lilac color with a peak emission wavelength of 766.5 nanometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neutral potassium atoms have 19 electrons, one more than the extremely stable configuration of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. Because of this and its low first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a> of 418.8 kJ/mol, the potassium atom is much more likely to lose the last electron and acquire a positive charge than to gain one and acquire a negative charge (though negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Alkalide" title="Alkalide">alkalide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions are not impossible).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K-_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-K--17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K+++_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-K+++-18">[18]</a></sup> This process requires so little energy that potassium is readily oxidized by atmospheric oxygen. In contrast, the second ionization energy is very high (3052 kJ/mol), because removal of two electrons breaks the stable noble gas electronic configuration (the configuration of the inert argon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K+++_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-K+++-18">[18]</a></sup> Potassium therefore does not form compounds with the oxidation state of +2 or higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K-_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-K--17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Potassium is an extremely active metal that reacts violently with oxygen in water and air. With oxygen it forms <a href="/wiki/Potassium_peroxide" title="Potassium peroxide">potassium peroxide</a>, and with water potassium forms <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>. The reaction of potassium with water is dangerous because of its violent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> character and the production of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas. Hydrogen reacts again with atmospheric oxygen, producing water, which reacts with the remaining potassium. This reaction requires only traces of water; because of this, potassium and the liquid sodium-potassium (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NaK" title="NaK">NaK</a>) alloy are potent <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccants</a> that can be used to dry <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a> prior to distillation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b35_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-b35-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the sensitivity of potassium to water and air, reactions with other elements are possible only in an inert atmosphere such as <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> gas using <a href="/wiki/Air-free_technique" title="Air-free technique">air-free techniques</a>. Potassium does not react with most hydrocarbons such as mineral oil or <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-20">[20]</a></sup> It readily dissolves in liquid <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, up to 480 g per 1000 g of ammonia at 0 °C. Depending on the concentration, the ammonia solutions are blue to yellow, and their electrical conductivity is similar to that of liquid metals. In a pure solution, potassium slowly reacts with ammonia to form <span class="chemf nowrap">KNH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, but this reaction is accelerated by minute amounts of transition metal salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b32_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-b32-21">[21]</a></sup> Because it can reduce the <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> to the metal, potassium is often used as the reductant in the preparation of finely divided metals from their salts by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rieke_metal" title="Rieke metal">Rieke method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> For example, the preparation of magnesium by this method employs potassium as the reductant:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">MgCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></a></span>  +  2 K  →  Mg  +  2 KCl</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dd><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="973" data-file-width="1100" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png/170px-Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png/255px-Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png/340px-Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potassium-superoxide-unit-cell-3D-ionic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of solid potassium superoxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">KO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>).</div></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>The only common oxidation state for potassium is +1. Potassium metal is a powerful <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> that is easily oxidized to the monopositive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cation</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Once oxidized, it is very stable and difficult to reduce back to the metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K-_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-K--17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Potassium oxidizes faster than most metals and often forms <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> containing oxygen-oxygen bonds, as do all alkali metals except lithium. There are three possible oxides of potassium: <a href="/wiki/Potassium_oxide" title="Potassium oxide">potassium oxide</a> (K<sub>2</sub>O), <a href="/wiki/Potassium_peroxide" title="Potassium peroxide">potassium peroxide</a> (K<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_superoxide" title="Potassium superoxide">potassium superoxide</a> (KO<sub>2</sub>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> they contain three different oxygen-based ions: <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). The latter two species, especially the <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a>, are rare and are formed only in reaction of very <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electropositive</a> metals (Na, K, Rb, Cs, etc.) with oxygen; these species contain oxygen-oxygen bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b32_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-b32-21">[21]</a></sup> All potassium-oxygen binary compounds are known to react with water violently, forming <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>.
</p><p>Potassium hydroxide (KOH) is a very strong alkali, and up to 1.21 <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a> of it can dissolve in merely one liter of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> KOH reacts readily with carbon dioxide to produce <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a>, and is used to remove traces of the gas from air.
</p><p>In general, potassium compounds are highly ionic and, owing to the high hydration energy of the <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion, have excellent water solubility. The main species in water solution are the aquated complexes <span class="chemf nowrap">[K(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> where n = 6 and 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lincoln_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lincoln-26">[26]</a></sup> The potassium ion is colorless in water and is very difficult to <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a>; possible precipitation methods include reactions with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_tetraphenylborate" title="Sodium tetraphenylborate">sodium tetraphenylborate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexachloroplatinic_acid" title="Hexachloroplatinic acid">hexachloroplatinic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_cobaltinitrite" title="Sodium cobaltinitrite">sodium cobaltinitrite</a> into <a href="/wiki/Potassium_tetraphenylborate" title="Potassium tetraphenylborate">potassium tetraphenylborate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hexachloroplatinate" title="Potassium hexachloroplatinate">potassium hexachloroplatinate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cobaltinitrite" title="Potassium cobaltinitrite">potassium cobaltinitrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_potassium" title="Isotopes of potassium">Isotopes of potassium</a></div>
<p>There are 24 known <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of potassium, three of which occur naturally: <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">39</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> (93.3%), <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> (0.0117%), and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">41</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> (6.7%). Naturally occurring <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</a></span> has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 1.250×10<sup>9</sup> years. It decays to stable <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</a></span> by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a> (11.2%) or to stable <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ca</a></span> by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> (88.8%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-27">[27]</a></sup> The decay of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> is the basis of a common method for dating rocks. The conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potassium-argon_dating" title="Potassium-argon dating">K-Ar dating method</a> depends on the assumption that the rocks contained no argon at the time of formation and that all the subsequent radiogenic argon (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span>) was quantitatively retained. <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Minerals</a> are dated by measurement of the concentration of potassium and the amount of radiogenic <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ar</span> that has accumulated. The minerals best suited for dating include <a href="/wiki/Biotite" title="Biotite">biotite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscovite" title="Muscovite">muscovite</a>, metamorphic <a href="/wiki/Hornblende" title="Hornblende">hornblende</a>, and volcanic <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspar</a>; <a href="/wiki/Petrography" title="Petrography">whole rock</a> samples from volcanic flows and shallow <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">instrusives</a> can also be dated if they are unaltered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Apart from dating, potassium isotopes have been used as <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">tracers</a> in studies of <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> and for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycling" title="Nutrient cycling">nutrient cycling</a> studies because potassium is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macronutrient_(ecology)" title="Macronutrient (ecology)">macronutrient</a> required for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> occurs in natural potassium (and thus in some commercial salt substitutes) in sufficient quantity that large bags of those substitutes can be used as a radioactive source for classroom demonstrations. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> is the radioisotope with the largest abundance in the body. In healthy animals and people, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> represents the largest source of radioactivity, greater even than <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">14</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>C</a></span>. In a human body of 70 kg mass, about 4,400 nuclei of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> decay per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The activity of natural potassium is 31 <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a>/g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_formation_and_distribution">Cosmic formation and distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cosmic formation and distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="307" data-file-width="360" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg/170px-PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg/255px-PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg/340px-PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PotassiumFeldsparUSGOV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potassium in <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspar</a></div></div></div>
<p>Potassium is formed in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">Supernovae</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> from lighter atoms. Potassium is principally created in Type II supernovae via an <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">explosive oxygen-burning process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K</span> is also formed in <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> nucleosynthesis and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neon_burning_process" title="Neon burning process">neon burning process</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Potassium is the 20th most abundant element in the solar system and the 17th most abundant element by weight in the earth. It makes up about 2.6% of the weight of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">earth's crust</a> and is the seventh most abundant element in the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>   The potassium concentration in seawater is 0.39 g/L<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seawaterconcentration_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-seawaterconcentration-5">[5]</a></sup> (0.039 wt/v%), about one twenty-seventh the concentration of sodium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geo_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-geo-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indus_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-indus-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potash">Potash</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Potash">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">Potash</a></div>
<p>Potash is primarily a mixture of potassium salts because plants have little or no sodium content, and the rest of a plant's major mineral content consists of calcium salts of relatively low solubility in water. While potash has been used since ancient times, it was not understood for most of its history to be a fundamentally different substance from sodium mineral salts. <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ernst_Stahl" title="Georg Ernst Stahl">Georg Ernst Stahl</a> obtained experimental evidence that led him to suggest the fundamental difference of sodium and potassium salts in 1702,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1702Suspect_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-1702Suspect-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Louis_Duhamel_du_Monceau" title="Henri Louis Duhamel du Monceau">Henri Louis Duhamel du Monceau</a> was able to prove this difference in 1736.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The exact chemical composition of potassium and sodium compounds, and the status as chemical element of potassium and sodium, was not known then, and thus <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> did not include the alkali in his list of chemical elements in 1789.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weeks_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-weeks-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disco_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-disco-38">[38]</a></sup> For a long time the only significant applications for potash were the production of glass, bleach, soap and <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> as potassium nitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Potassium soaps from animal fats and vegetable oils were especially prized because they tend to be more water-soluble and of softer texture, and are therefore known as soft <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soaps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g73_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-g73-8">[8]</a></sup> The discovery by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justus_Liebig" title="Justus Liebig">Justus Liebig</a> in 1840 that potassium is a necessary element for plants and that most types of soil lack potassium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> caused a steep rise in demand for potassium salts. Wood-ash from fir trees was initially used as a potassium salt source for fertilizer, but, with the discovery in 1868 of mineral deposits containing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> near <a href="/wiki/Sta%C3%9Ffurt" title="Staßfurt">Staßfurt</a>, Germany, the production of potassium-containing fertilizers began at an industrial scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Other potash deposits were discovered, and by the 1960s Canada became the dominant producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal">Metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Humphry_Davy,_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3123" data-file-width="2400" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/220px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/330px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/440px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Humphry_Davy,_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potassium.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Potassium.JPG/220px-Potassium.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Potassium.JPG/330px-Potassium.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Potassium.JPG/440px-Potassium.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potassium.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pieces of potassium metal</div></div></div>
<p>Potassium <i>metal</i> was first isolated in 1807 by Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, who derived it from <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">caustic potash</a> (KOH, potassium hydroxide) by electrolysis of molten KOH with the newly discovered <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">voltaic pile</a>. Potassium was the first metal that was isolated by electrolysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enghag2004_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enghag2004-46">[46]</a></sup> Later in the same year, Davy reported extraction of the metal <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> from a mineral derivative (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caustic_soda" title="Caustic soda">caustic soda</a>, NaOH, or lye) rather than a plant salt, by a similar technique, demonstrating that the elements, and thus the salts, are different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weeks_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-weeks-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disco_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-disco-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy1807_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1807-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200disco_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-200disco-48">[48]</a></sup> Although the production of potassium and sodium metal should have shown that both are elements, it took some time before this view was universally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disco_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-disco-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Elemental potassium does not occur in nature because of its high reactivity. It reacts violently with water (see section Precautions below)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-20">[20]</a></sup> and also reacts with oxygen. <a href="/wiki/Orthoclase" title="Orthoclase">Orthoclase</a> (potassium feldspar) is a common rock-forming mineral. <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">Granite</a> for example contains 5% potassium, which is well above the average in the Earth's crust. <a href="/wiki/Sylvite" title="Sylvite">Sylvite</a> (KCl), <a href="/wiki/Carnallite" title="Carnallite">carnallite</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">(KCl·MgCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>·6(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O))</span>, <a href="/wiki/Kainite" title="Kainite">kainite</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">(MgSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·KCl·3H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)</span> and <a href="/wiki/Langbeinite" title="Langbeinite">langbeinite</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">(MgSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)</span> are the minerals found in large <a href="/wiki/Evaporite" title="Evaporite">evaporite</a> deposits worldwide. The deposits often show layers starting with the least soluble at the bottom and the most soluble on top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indus_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-indus-35">[35]</a></sup> Deposits of niter (<a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>) are formed by decomposition of organic material in contact with atmosphere, mostly in caves; because of the good water solubility of niter the formation of larger deposits requires special environmental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Potassium_in_biology" title="Potassium in biology">Potassium in biology</a></div>
<p>Potassium is the eighth or ninth most common element by mass (0.2%) in the human body, so that a 60 kg adult contains a total of about 120 g of potassium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">body</a> has about as much potassium as sulfur and chlorine, and only calcium and phosphorus are more abundant (with the exception of the ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/CHON" title="CHON">CHON</a> elements).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Potassium ions are present in a wide variety of proteins and enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_function">Biochemical function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Biochemical function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Potassium levels influence multiple physiological processes, including<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>resting cellular-membrane potential and the propagation of action potentials in neuronal, muscular, and cardiac tissue. Due to the electrostatic and chemical properties, <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions are larger than <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions, and ion channels and pumps in cell membranes can differentiate between the two ions, actively pumping or passively passing one of the two ions while blocking the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>hormone secretion and action</li>
<li>vascular tone</li>
<li>systemic blood pressure control</li>
<li>gastrointestinal motility</li>
<li>acid–base homeostasis</li>
<li>glucose and insulin metabolism</li>
<li>mineralocorticoid action</li>
<li>renal concentrating ability</li>
<li>fluid and electrolyte balance</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homeostasis">Homeostasis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Homeostasis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Potassium homeostasis denotes the maintenance of the total body potassium content, plasma potassium level, and the ratio of the intracellular to extracellular potassium concentrations within narrow limits, in the face of pulsatile intake (meals), obligatory renal excretion, and shifts between intracellular and extracellular compartments.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_levels">Plasma levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Plasma levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Plasma potassium is normally kept at 3.5 to 5.0 millimoles (mmol) [or milliequivalents (mEq)] per liter by multiple mechanisms. Levels outside this range are associated with an increasing rate of death from multiple causes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and some cardiac, kidney,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and lung diseases progress more rapidly if serum potassium levels are not maintained within the normal range.
</p><p>An average meal of 40-50 mmol presents the body with more potassium than is present in all plasma (20-25 mmol). However, this surge causes the plasma potassium to rise only 10% at most as a result of prompt and efficient clearance by both renal and extra-renal mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hypokalemia" title="Hypokalemia">Hypokalemia</a>, a deficiency of potassium in the plasma, can be fatal if severe. Common causes are increased gastrintestinal loss (<a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>), and increased renal loss (<a href="/wiki/Polyuria" title="Polyuria">diuresis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Deficiency symptoms include muscle weakness, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralytic_ileus" title="Paralytic ileus">paralytic ileus</a>, ECG abnormalities, decreased reflex response; and in severe cases, respiratory paralysis, <a href="/wiki/Alkalosis" title="Alkalosis">alkalosis</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">cardiac arrhythmia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_mechanisms">Control mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Control mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Potassium content in the plasma is tightly controlled by four basic mechanisms, which have various names and classifications.  The four are 1) a reactive negative-feedback system, 2) a reactive feed-forward system, 3) a predictive or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circadian" title="Circadian">circadian</a> system, and 4) an internal  or cell membrane transport system. Collectively, the first three are sometimes termed the "external potassium homeostasis system";<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title='"internal'>citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the first two, the "reactive potassium homeostasis system".
</p>
<ul><li>The reactive negative-feedback system refers to the system that induces renal secretion of potassium in response to a rise in the plasma potassium (potassium ingestion, shift out of cells, or intravenous infusion.)</li>
<li>The reactive feed-forward system refers to an incompletely understood system that induces renal potassium secretion in response to potassium ingestion prior to any rise in the plasma potassium. This is probably initiated by gut cell potassium receptors that detect ingested potassium and trigger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vagal" title="Vagal">vagal</a> <a href="/wiki/Afferent_nerve_fiber" title="Afferent nerve fiber">afferent</a> signals to the pituitary gland.</li>
<li>The predictive or circadian system increases renal secretion of potassium during mealtime hours (e.g. daytime for humans, nighttime for rodents) independent of the presence, amount, or absence of potassium ingestion. It is mediated by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circadian_oscillator" title="Circadian oscillator">circadian oscillator</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nucleus" title="Suprachiasmatic nucleus">suprachiasmatic nucleus</a> of the brain (central clock), which causes the kidney (peripheral clock) to secrete potassium in this rhythmic circadian fashion.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="635" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg/400px-Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg/600px-Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg/800px-Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_sodium-potassium_pump-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The action of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium-potassium_pump" title="Sodium-potassium pump">sodium-potassium pump</a> is an example of primary <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a>. The two carrier proteins embedded in the cell membrane on the left are using <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> to move sodium out of the cell against the concentration gradient; The two proteins on the right are using secondary active transport to move potassium into the cell: this process results in reconstitution of ATP.</div></div></div></li>
<li>The ion transport system moves potassium across the cell membrane using two mechanisms. One is active and pumps sodium out of, and potassium into, the cell. The other is passive and allows potassium to leak out of the cell. Potassium and sodium cations influence fluid distribution between intracellular and extracellular compartments by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osmotic" title="Osmotic">osmotic</a> forces. The movement of potassium and sodium through the cell membrane is mediated by the <a href="/wiki/Na%2B/K%2B-ATPase" title="Na+/K+-ATPase">Na+/K+-ATPase</a> pump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Ion_transporter" title="Ion transporter">ion pump</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> to pump three sodium ions out of the cell and two potassium ions into the cell, creating an electrochemical gradient and electromotive force across the cell membrane. The highly selective <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potassium_ion_channels" title="Potassium ion channels">potassium ion channels</a> (which are <a href="/wiki/Tetramer" title="Tetramer">tetramers</a>) are crucial for <a href="/wiki/Hyperpolarization_(biology)" title="Hyperpolarization (biology)">hyperpolarization</a> inside <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> after an action potential is triggered, to cite one example. The most recently discovered potassium ion channel is KirBac3.1, which makes a total of five potassium ion channels (KcsA, KirBac1.1, KirBac3.1, KvAP, and MthK) with a determined structure. All five are from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16253415_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16253415-63">[63]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span id="Renal_filtration.2C_reabsorption.2C_and_excretion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Renal_filtration,_reabsorption,_and_excretion">Renal filtration, reabsorption, and excretion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Renal filtration, reabsorption, and excretion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Renal handling of potassium is closely connected to sodium handling. Potassium is the major cation (positive ion) inside animal cells [150 mmol/L, (4.8 g)], while sodium is the major cation of extracellular fluid [150 mmol/L, (3.345 g)]. In the kidneys, about 180 liters of plasma is filtered through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glomeruli" title="Glomeruli">glomeruli</a> and into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renal_tubules" title="Renal tubules">renal tubules</a> per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Potts1964_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Potts1964-64">[64]</a></sup> This filtering involves about 600 g of sodium and 33 g of potassium. Since only 1–10 g of sodium and 1–4 g of potassium are likely to be replaced by diet, renal filtering must efficiently reabsorb the remainder from the plasma.
</p><p>Sodium is reabsorbed to maintain extracellular volume, osmotic pressure, and serum sodium concentration within narrow limits; potassium is reabsorbed to maintain serum potassium concentration within narrow limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_pump" title="Sodium pump">Sodium pumps</a> in the renal tubules operate to reabsorb sodium. Potassium must be conserved also, but, because the amount of potassium in the blood plasma is very small and the pool of potassium in the cells is about thirty times as large, the situation is not so critical for potassium. Since potassium is moved passively<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> in counter flow to sodium in response to an apparent (but not actual) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donnan_equilibrium" title="Donnan equilibrium">Donnan equilibrium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> the urine can never sink below the concentration of potassium in serum except sometimes by actively excreting water at the end of the processing. Potassium is excreted twice and reabsorbed three times before the urine reaches the collecting tubules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> At that point, urine usually has about the same potassium concentration as plasma. At the end of the processing, potassium is secreted one more time if the serum levels are too high.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>With no potassium intake, it is excreted at about 200 mg per day until, in about a week, potassium in the serum declines to a mildly deficient level of 3.0–3.5 mmol/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>  If potassium is still withheld, the concentration continues to fall until a severe deficiency causes eventual death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The potassium moves passively through pores in the cell membrane. When ions move through pumps there is a gate in the pumps on either side of the cell membrane and only one gate can be open at once. As a result, approximately 100 ions are forced through per second. Pores have only one gate, and there only one kind of ion can stream through, at 10 million to 100 million ions per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The pores require calcium to open<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> although it is thought that the calcium works in reverse by blocking at least one of the pores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Carbonyl groups inside the pore on the amino acids mimic the water hydration that takes place in water solution<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> by the nature of the electrostatic charges on four carbonyl groups inside the pore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Dietary recommendations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The U.S. Institute of Medicine (IOM) sets Estimated Average Requirements (EARs) and Recommended Dietary Allowances (RDAs), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adequate_Intake" title="Adequate Intake">Adequate Intakes</a> (AIs) for when there is not sufficient information to set EARs and RDAs. Collectively the EARs, RDAs, AIs and ULs are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intakes</a>. The AIs for potassium are: 400 mg of potassium for 0-6-month-old males, 700 mg of potassium for 7-12-month-old males, 3,000 mg of potassium for 1-3-year-old males, 3,800 mg of potassium for 4-8-year-old males, 4,500 mg of potassium for 9-13-year-old males, and 4,700 mg of potassium for males that are 14 years old and older.
The AIs for potassium are: 400 mg of potassium for 0-6-month-old females, 700 mg of potassium for 7-12-month-old females, 3,000 mg of potassium for 1-3-year-old females, 3,800 mg of potassium for 4-8-year-old females, 4,500 mg of potassium for 9-13-year-old females, and 4,700 mg of potassium for females that are 14 years old and older.
The AIs for potassium are: 4,700 mg of potassium for 14-50-year-old pregnant females; furthermore, 5,100 mg of potassium for 14-50-year-old lactating females. As for safety, the IOM also sets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" title="Tolerable upper intake level">Tolerable upper intake levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals, but for potassium the evidence was insufficient, so no UL established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Americans consume only half that amount per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iom_panel2005_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-iom_panel2005-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Likewise, in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, in particular in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, insufficient potassium intake is somewhat common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>  However, the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">British National Health Service</a> recommends a lower intake, saying that adults need 3,500 mg per day and that excess amounts may cause health problems such as stomach pain and diarrhoea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_sources">Food sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Food sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Potassium is present in all fruits, vegetables, meat and fish. Foods with high potassium concentrations include <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parsley" title="Parsley">parsley</a>, dried <a href="/wiki/Apricot" title="Apricot">apricots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>, all <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">almonds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pistachio" title="Pistachio">pistachios</a>), <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_shoot" title="Bamboo shoot">bamboo shoots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocados</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coconut_water" title="Coconut water">coconut water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> lists <a href="/wiki/Tomato_paste" title="Tomato paste">tomato paste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_juice" title="Orange juice">orange juice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beet_greens" title="Beet greens">beet greens</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_beans" title="White beans">white beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">plantains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, apricots, and many other dietary sources of potassium, ranked in descending order according to potassium content. A day's worth of potassium is in 5 plantains or 11 bananas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficient_intake">Deficient intake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Deficient intake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diets low in potassium can lead to <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hypokalemia" title="Hypokalemia">hypokalemia</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supplementation">Supplementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Supplementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Supplements of potassium are most widely used in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Diuretic" title="Diuretic">diuretics</a> that block reabsorption of sodium and water upstream from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distal_tubule" title="Distal tubule">distal tubule</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thiazide" title="Thiazide">thiazides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loop_diuretics" title="Loop diuretics">loop diuretics</a>), because this promotes increased distal tubular potassium secretion, with resultant increased potassium excretion. A variety of prescription and over-the counter supplements are available.  Potassium chloride may be dissolved in water, but the salty/bitter taste make liquid supplements unpalatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitter_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-bitter-84">[84]</a></sup> Typical doses range from 10 mmol (400 mg), to 20 mmol (800 mg).  Potassium is also available in tablets or capsules, which are formulated to allow potassium to leach slowly out of a matrix, since very high concentrations of potassium ion that occur adjacent to a solid tablet can injure the gastric or intestinal mucosa. For this reason, non-prescription potassium pills are limited by law in the US to a maximum of 99 mg of potassium.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Since the kidneys are the site of potassium excretion, individuals with impaired kidney function are at risk for <a href="/wiki/Hyperkalemia" title="Hyperkalemia">hyperkalemia</a> if dietary potassium and supplements are not restricted. The more severe the impairment, the more severe is the restriction necessary to avoid hyperkalemia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> concluded that a 1640 mg increase in the daily intake of potassium was associated with a 21% lower risk of stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">Potassium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bicarbonate" title="Potassium bicarbonate">potassium bicarbonate</a> may be useful to control mild <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_by_taste_buds">Detection by taste buds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Detection by taste buds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Potassium can be detected by taste because it triggers three of the five types of taste sensations, according to concentration. Dilute solutions of potassium ions taste sweet, allowing moderate concentrations in milk and juices, while higher concentrations become increasingly bitter/alkaline, and finally also salty to the taste. The combined bitterness and saltiness of high-potassium solutions makes high-dose potassium supplementation by liquid drinks a palatability challenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitter_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-bitter-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_production">Commercial production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Commercial production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1383" data-file-width="2075" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg/220px-Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg/330px-Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg/440px-Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museo_de_La_Plata_-_Silvita.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sylvite" title="Sylvite">Sylvite</a> from New Mexico</div></div></div>
<p>Potassium salts such as <a href="/wiki/Carnallite" title="Carnallite">carnallite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Langbeinite" title="Langbeinite">langbeinite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyhalite" title="Polyhalite">polyhalite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sylvite" title="Sylvite">sylvite</a> form extensive <a href="/wiki/Evaporite" title="Evaporite">evaporite</a> deposits in ancient lake bottoms and <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">seabeds</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geo_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-geo-34">[34]</a></sup> making extraction of potassium salts in these environments commercially viable. The principal source of potassium – <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a> – is mined in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, and other places around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSCS2008_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2008-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSYB2006_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSYB2006-90">[90]</a></sup> The first mined deposits were located near Staßfurt, Germany, but the deposits span from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> over Germany into Poland. They are located in the <a href="/wiki/Zechstein" title="Zechstein">Zechstein</a> and were deposited in the Middle to Late <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>. The largest deposits ever found lie 1,000 meters (3,300 feet) below the surface of the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>. The deposits are located in the <a href="/wiki/Elk_Point_Group" title="Elk Point Group">Elk Point Group</a> produced in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Devonian" title="Middle Devonian">Middle Devonian</a>. Saskatchewan, where several large mines have operated since the 1960s pioneered the technique of freezing of wet sands (the Blairmore formation) to drive mine shafts through them.
