The main potash mining company in Saskatchewan is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potash_Corporation_of_Saskatchewan" title="Potash Corporation of Saskatchewan">Potash Corporation of Saskatchewan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The water of the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a> is used by Israel and Jordan as a source of potash, while the concentration in normal oceans is too low for commercial production at current prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSCS2008_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2008-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSYB2006_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGSYB2006-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg/220px-Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg/330px-Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg/440px-Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wintershall_Monte_Kali_12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Monte_Kali_(Heringen)" title="Monte Kali (Heringen)">Monte Kali</a>, a potash mining and <a href="/wiki/Beneficiation" title="Beneficiation">beneficiation</a> waste heap in <a href="/wiki/Hesse" title="Hesse">Hesse, Germany</a>, consisting mostly of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_extraction">Chemical extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chemical extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several methods are used to separate potassium salts from sodium and magnesium compounds. The most-used method is fractional precipitation using the solubility differences of the salts at different temperatures. Electrostatic separation of the ground salt mixture is also used in some mines. The resulting sodium and magnesium waste is either stored underground or piled up in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slag_heap" title="Slag heap">slag heaps</a>. Most of the mined potassium mineral ends up as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> after processing. The mineral industry refers to potassium chloride either as potash, muriate of potash, or simply MOP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indus_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-indus-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure potassium metal can be isolated by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of its <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">hydroxide</a> in a process that has changed little since it was first used by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> in 1807. Although the electrolysis process was developed and used in industrial scale in the 1920s, the thermal method by reacting sodium with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> in a chemical equilibrium reaction became the dominant method in the 1950s.
</p><p>The production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NaK" title="NaK">sodium potassium alloys</a> is accomplished by changing the reaction time and the amount of sodium used in the reaction. The Griesheimer process employing the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">potassium fluoride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbide" title="Calcium carbide">calcium carbide</a> was also used to produce potassium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indus_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-indus-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Na + KCl → NaCl + K                      (Thermal method)</dd>
<dd>2 KF + <span class="chemf nowrap">CaC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → 2 K + <span class="chemf nowrap">CaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 C    (Griesheimer process)</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">Reagent-grade</a> potassium metal costs about $10.00/<a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a> ($22/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kg" title="Kg">kg</a>) in 2010 when purchased by the <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a>. Lower purity metal is considerably cheaper. The market is volatile because long-term storage of the metal is difficult. It must be stored in a dry <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> atmosphere or <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oil</a> to prevent the formation of a surface layer of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_superoxide" title="Potassium superoxide">potassium superoxide</a>, a pressure-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosive</a> that <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonates</a> when scratched.  The resulting explosion often starts a fire difficult to extinguish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fire_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-fire-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_uses">Commercial uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Commercial uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizer">Fertilizer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Fertilizer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patentkali_(Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1316" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg/220px-Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg/330px-Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg/440px-Patentkali_%28Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patentkali_(Potassium_sulfate_with_magnesium).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potassium sulfate/magnesium sulfate fertilizer</div></div></div>
<p>Potassium ions are an essential component of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> nutrition and are found in most <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g73_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-g73-8">[8]</a></sup> They are used as a <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroponic" title="Hydroponic">hydroponic</a> culture in the form of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">chloride</a> (KCl), <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfate" title="Potassium sulfate">sulfate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">nitrate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KNO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>). Agricultural fertilizers consume 95% of global potassium chemical production, and about 90% of this potassium is supplied as KCl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g73_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-g73-8">[8]</a></sup> The potassium content of most plants range from 0.5% to 2% of the harvested weight of crops, conventionally expressed as amount of <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. Modern high-<a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">yield</a> agriculture depends upon fertilizers to replace the potassium lost at harvest. Most agricultural fertilizers contain potassium chloride, while potassium sulfate is used for chloride-sensitive crops or crops needing higher sulfur content. The sulfate is produced mostly by decomposition of the complex minerals <a href="/wiki/Kainite" title="Kainite">kainite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">MgSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·KCl·3H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) and <a href="/wiki/Langbeinite" title="Langbeinite">langbeinite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">MgSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). Only a very few fertilizers contain potassium nitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent-95">[95]</a></sup> In 2005, about 93% of world potassium production was consumed by the fertilizer industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSYB2006_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGSYB2006-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_additives">Food additives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Food additives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Potassium sodium tartrate (<span class="chemf nowrap">KNaC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rochelle_salt" title="Rochelle salt">Rochelle salt</a>) is the main constituent of <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a>; it is also used in the <a href="/wiki/Silvering" title="Silvering">silvering</a> of mirrors. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bromate" title="Potassium bromate">Potassium bromate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KBrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) is a strong oxidizer (E924), used to improve dough strength and rise height. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bisulfite" title="Potassium bisulfite">Potassium bisulfite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KHSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) is used as a food preservative, for example in <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>-making (but not in meats). It is also used to <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a> textiles and straw, and in the tanning of <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leathers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Major potassium chemicals are potassium hydroxide, potassium carbonate, potassium sulfate, and potassium chloride. Megatons of these compounds are produced annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">Potassium hydroxide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">KOH</span> is a strong base, which is used in industry to neutralize strong and weak <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, to control <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and to manufacture potassium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>. It is also used to <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponify</a> <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oils" title="Oils">oils</a>, in industrial cleaners, and in <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> reactions, for example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esters" title="Esters">esters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">Potassium nitrate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KNO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) or saltpeter is obtained from natural sources such as <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporites" title="Evaporites">evaporites</a> or manufactured via the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>; it is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidant" title="Oxidant">oxidant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_powder" title="Black powder">black powder</a>) and an important agricultural fertilizer. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">Potassium cyanide</a> (KCN) is used industrially to dissolve <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and precious metals, in particular <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, by forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">complexes</a>. Its applications include <a href="/wiki/Gold_mining" title="Gold mining">gold mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electroforming" title="Electroforming">electroforming</a> of these <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>; it is also used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a> to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitriles" title="Nitriles">nitriles</a>. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">Potassium carbonate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> or potash) is used in the manufacture of glass, soap, color TV tubes, fluorescent lamps, textile dyes and pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Potassium permanganate (<span class="chemf nowrap">KMnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) is an oxidizing, bleaching and purification substance and is used for production of <a href="/wiki/Saccharin" title="Saccharin">saccharin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chlorate" title="Potassium chlorate">Potassium chlorate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KClO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) is added to matches and explosives. <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bromide" title="Potassium bromide">Potassium bromide</a> (KBr) was formerly used as a sedative and in photography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g73_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-g73-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Potassium_chromate" title="Potassium chromate">Potassium chromate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) is used in <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">inks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stain" title="Stain">stains</a> (bright yellowish-red color); in <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a>; in the tanning of leather, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fly_paper" title="Fly paper">fly paper</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safety_match" title="Safety match">safety matches</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> but all these uses are due to the chemistry of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromate" title="Chromate">chromate</a> ion, rather than the potassium ion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche_uses">Niche uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Niche uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are thousands of uses of various potassium compounds. One example is <a href="/wiki/Potassium_superoxide" title="Potassium superoxide">potassium superoxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">KO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, an orange solid that acts as a portable source of oxygen and a carbon dioxide absorber. It is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Rebreather#Rebreathers_whose_absorbent_releases_oxygen" title="Rebreather">respiration systems</a> in mines, submarines and spacecraft as it takes less volume than the gaseous oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 <span class="chemf nowrap">KO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 CO<sub>2</sub> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Another example is <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cobaltinitrite" title="Potassium cobaltinitrite">potassium cobaltinitrite</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>[Co(NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]</span>, which is used as artist's pigment under the name of <a href="/wiki/Aureolin" title="Aureolin">Aureolin</a> or Cobalt Yellow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Getts_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Getts-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The stable isotopes of potassium can be <a href="/wiki/Laser_cooling" title="Laser cooling">laser cooled</a> and used to probe fundamental and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_technology" title="Quantum technology">technological</a> problems in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum physics</a>. The two <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosonic</a> isotopes possess convenient <a href="/wiki/Feshbach_resonance" title="Feshbach resonance">Feshbach resonances</a> to enable studies requiring tunable interactions, while <sup>40</sup>K is one of only two stable <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> amongst the alkali metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_uses">Laboratory uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Laboratory uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> of sodium and potassium, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NaK" title="NaK">NaK</a> is a liquid used as a heat-transfer medium and a <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccant</a> for producing <a href="/wiki/Air-free_technique" title="Air-free technique">dry and air-free solvents</a>. It can also be used in <a href="/wiki/Reactive_distillation" title="Reactive distillation">reactive distillation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The ternary alloy of 12% Na, 47% K and 41% Cs has the lowest melting point of −78 °C of any metallic compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g76_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-g76-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metallic potassium is used in several types of <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg" title="The flame pictogram in the Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals (GHS)"><img alt="The flame pictogram in the Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals (GHS)" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="724" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg/50px-GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg/75px-GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg/100px-GHS-pictogram-flamme.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GHS-pictogram-acid.svg" title="The corrosion pictogram in the Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals (GHS)"><img alt="The corrosion pictogram in the Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals (GHS)" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="724" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GHS-pictogram-acid.svg/50px-GHS-pictogram-acid.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GHS-pictogram-acid.svg/75px-GHS-pictogram-acid.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GHS-pictogram-acid.svg/100px-GHS-pictogram-acid.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Globally_Harmonized_System_of_Classification_and_Labelling_of_Chemicals" title="Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals">GHS signal word</a>
</td>
<td>Danger
</td></tr>
<tr>

<td><abbr class="abbr" title="In contact with water releases flammable gases which may ignite spontaneously">H260</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Causes severe skin burns and eye damage">H314</abbr>
</td></tr>

<td><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/GHS_precautionary_statements" title="GHS precautionary statements">GHS precautionary statements</a></div>

Hydrogen is produced, and with potassium vapor, burns with a pink or lilac flame. Strongly alkaline potassium hydroxide is formed in solution.</div></div></div>
<p>Potassium metal reacts violently with water producing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a> (KOH) and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas.
</p>
<dl><dd>2 K (s) + 2 <span class="Unicode"> </span><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">H<sub>2</sub>O</a> (l) → 2 KOH (aq) + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>↑ (g)</dd></dl>
<p>This reaction is exothermic and releases enough heat to ignite the resulting hydrogen in the presence of oxygen, possibly explosively splashing onlookers with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, which is a strong <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> that destroys living tissue and causes skin burns. Finely grated potassium ignites in air at room temperature. The bulk metal ignites in air if heated. Because its density is 0.89 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, burning potassium floats in water that exposes it to atmospheric oxygen. Many common fire extinguishing agents, including water, either are ineffective or make a potassium fire worse. <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">Nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (table salt), <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> (soda ash), and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (sand) are effective if they are dry. Some <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher" title="Fire extinguisher">Class D</a> dry powder extinguishers designed for metal fires are also effective. These agents deprive the fire of oxygen and cool the potassium metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Potassium reacts violently with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halogens" title="Halogens">halogens</a> and detonates in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>. It also reacts explosively with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. During combustion, potassium forms peroxides and superoxides. These peroxides may react violently with <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> such as oils. Both peroxides and superoxides may react explosively with metallic potassium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because potassium reacts with water vapor in the air, it is usually stored under anhydrous mineral oil or kerosene. Unlike lithium and sodium, however, potassium should not be stored under oil for longer than six months, unless in an inert (oxygen free) atmosphere, or under vacuum. After prolonged storage in air dangerous shock-sensitive peroxides can form on the metal and under the lid of the container, and can detonate upon opening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the highly reactive nature of potassium metal, it must be handled with great care, with full skin and eye protection and preferably an explosion-resistant barrier between the user and the metal. Ingestion of large amounts of potassium compounds can lead to <a href="/wiki/Hyperkalemia" title="Hyperkalemia">hyperkalemia</a>, strongly influencing the cardiovascular system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hyper_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-hyper-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Potassium chloride is used in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> for <a href="/wiki/Lethal_injection" title="Lethal injection">lethal injection</a> executions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hyper_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-hyper-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
